AWS AWS Google Cloud Google Cloud VMware VMware The ATC Blog Network Security Security +0 Blog • January 23, 2026 • 6 minute read A Quick look at QUIC Learn about QUIC (Quick UDP Internet Connection), a modern transport protocol that enhances internet speed, security and reliability by building on UDP. It integrates TLS 1.3 for faster handshakes, employs BBR for smarter congestion control, and uses multiplexed streams to eliminate head-of-line blocking. Seamless connection migration ensures uninterrupted connectivity, making QUIC a promising successor to TCP. In this blog A quick rundown on QUIC Download A quick rundown on QUIC QUIC is a modern internet transport protocol designed to make connections faster, more secure and reliable than traditional TCP, especially for today's web and mobile applications. What is QUIC? QUIC (Quick UDP Internet Connections) is a Layer 4 transport protocol that runs on top of UDP instead of TCP. It was originally created at Google by Jim Roskind around 2012 and standardized by the IETF as RFC 9000 in May 2021. By building on UDP, QUIC avoids some of TCP's built-in overhead while still handling key tasks like reliable delivery and retransmission of lost packets. In practice, this means QUIC aims to replace TCP as the default way browsers and many applications communicate with servers, especially as web traffic and performance expectations keep growing. Faster and stronger handshakes QUIC's handshake is both faster and stronger than TCP's because it natively incorporates TLS 1.3 into the transport, rather than layering encryption on afterward. Unlike TCP, which first performs the classic three-way handshake and then runs a TLS negotiation necessitating three round-trips, QUIC combines these steps so that cryptographic parameters and connection states are established together from the start. Modern TLS 1.3 features such as forward secrecy, robust cipher suites and streamlined key exchange are mandated with QUIC, while also ensuring that 0 and 1 RTT handshakes are enabled. The QUIC handshake also includes some measures to defend against DoS attacks targeting the handshake. Those measures are: Address Validation: Verification that an entity that claims an address can receive packets at that address. Without address validation, endpoints cannot send more than three times the data received from an unvalidated address. This measure prevents amplification attacks. Retry Packet: The Retry packet is a token-exchange mechanism that allows servers to validate a client's IP address before performing expensive signature and key-exchange computations. This prevents server-side DoS attacks that overwhelm the server with connection requests. Handshake Protection: Outside of dropping packets, on-path attackers cannot force handshake failure because handshake packets are protected with keys following the initial packet exchange. Cryptographic Protection: Both initial and later handshake packets are encrypted with per-version, and TLS exchanged derived keys, respectively. Taken together, these features make QUIC's handshake make for a more secure and resilient foundation that TCP plus TLS. From the very first packet, QUIC reduces setup latency and the window that attackers must hijack connections, thanks to TLS 1.3 and its built-in defenses. Smarter congestion control To prevent communication channels from becoming overloaded, TCP employs algorithms such as slow start, Reno, and Cubic to regulate data flow based on packet loss signals and RTT. That reactive behavior can underutilize available bandwidth or unnecessarily slow down transmission. Some of TCP's drawbacks are: TCP only reacts after congestion occurs, making it slow to respond and recover Packet loss can occur due to random network fluctuations rather than congestion. However, TCP will interpret the loss as congestion and unnecessarily slow down the transmission rate. TCP's congestion control does not scale efficiently in modern high-speed networks. As high-speed, low-latency applications become increasingly prevalent, TCP's limitations can no longer be ignored, and QUIC's enhanced congestion control mechanisms become necessary. The main congestion control algorithm used by QUIC is Bottleneck Bandwidth and RTT (BBR). BBR works by estimating the bandwidth and RTT of a network path. Based on that estimate, BBR calculates a transmission rate to fully utilize available capacity without overloading the network. By continuously updating its estimates as conditions change, BBR enables QUIC to effectively utilize modern high-speed internet connections. QUIC further builds on its improvements over TCP by using packet-level acknowledgments rather than sequence-based acknowledgments. With this change, QUIC is more precise and timelier with its loss detection and retransmission strategies, preventing unnecessary slowdowns in network performance. When a packet acknowledgment is not received, some QUIC extensions optionally utilize a forward error correction mechanism that sends repair data alongside typical packets, allowing the receiver to rebuild some lost packets immediately, without having to wait for them to be resent, crucial for applications that demand low-latency. Multiplexed streams without head-of-line blocking pain TCP delivers bytes in a strict order within a connection. As a result of that, if a packet is delayed or lost, everything after it must wait, even if those later packets arrived on time, in a scenario called head-of-line blocking. This scenario can spiral out of control, where packets that were blocked by a lost packet end up blocking packets themselves. QUIC solves this issue by utilizing independent multiplexed streams inside of a single connection: Each stream provides its own ordered byte stream, and streams can be uni- or bidirectional A connection can have many streams at once, each with its own ordering and reliability state If a packet is lost, only the affected stream pauses while it waits for retransmission; other streams keep delivering data normally. Multiplexed streams closely reflect how a modern webpage is built. A browser can put each image, script and API call on its own stream, so a delay in loading a large resource does not delay everything else on the page. This fine-grained separation gives devs more predictable performance and makes the overall experience smoother and more responsive. Seamless migration In a world always on the move, losing connection as you move between different IP addresses can be annoying, which is why QUIC's connection migration is a fantastic feature. QUIC supports seamless connection migration using stable connection IDs: After the handshake, the connection is primarily identified by its connection ID, not just IP and port. When the network path changes (from Wi-Fi to LTE), the client "probes" the new path and then shifts the existing connection to it. Stream, congestion control state, and encryption keys all carry over without a full reconnect or new handshake. To the application, this transition is mostly invisible, avoiding stalls, renegotiations, and extra latency that would occur with TCP during an IP change. This feature is especially powerful for mobile and Wi-Fi-heavy environments. A video call running over QUIC will continue smoothly as a phone leaves a cafés Wi-Fi and switches to cellular. The future is QUIC QUIC is an obvious solution for the TCP pain points that we have been dealing with for years. Through its use of TLS 1.3, independent multiplexed streams, and support for seamless migration across changing networks, QUIC can address head-of-line blocking, slow setup, and congestion management issues we see with TCP. As QUIC's adoption grows, its benefits will only become more widespread. fine-grained resource control API and SDK reference API Reference GTM API SDK Reference PrivateZone API reference Home Page Alibaba Cloud DNS Getting started Basic DNS concepts Basic DNS concepts Updated at: 2026-05-27 03:57:52 Copy as MD Learn key Alibaba Cloud DNS concepts including domain hierarchy, DNS resolution, caching, and TTL. DNS DNS (Domain Name System) translates domain names into IP addresses for both the Internet and corporate intranets. This process is called domain name resolution. As a distributed database, DNS lets users access resources by name instead of memorizing IP addresses. The DNS protocol runs over UDP on port 53. By network environment, DNS is categorized into public DNS and internal DNS. By functional role, it is categorized into authoritative DNS and recursive DNS. Domain hierarchy Domain names follow a hierarchical tree structure, with dot-separated labels forming a unique name for each Internet-connected host or router. Examples .com is a top-level domain name; aliyun.com is a primary domain name, also called a second-level domain; example.aliyun.com , www.aliyun.com are subdomains, also called third-level domains; test.example.aliyun.com is a subdomain of a subdomain, also called a fourth-level domain. DNS server hierarchy The DNS resolution process involves 4 levels of DNS servers, as follows: Server type Function Note Each domain level has a dedicated name server, with the root name server at the top level. Each layer stores the IP addresses of the next lower-level servers, enabling step-by-step resolution. DNS resolution process When you access a website using example.com , DNS resolution proceeds as follows. The user enters example.com in the browser, which queries the local DNS resolver. If the resolver has cached data for example.com , it returns the IP address directly (skip to step 9). Otherwise, it continues to step 2. The local DNS resolver queries the root name server. The root name server returns the address of the .com TLD name server to the local DNS resolver. The local DNS resolver sends a query request for example.com to the .com TLD name server. The .com TLD name server returns the address of the authoritative name server that provides authoritative resolution for example.com to the local DNS resolver. The local DNS resolver sends a query request to the authoritative name server. The authoritative name server returns the IP address corresponding to the domain name example.com to the local DNS resolver. The DNS resolver feeds the IP address into the browser. The Web browser accesses the website server using the IP address. The website server returns the webpage information. Recursive resolution (Recursive Query) A single DNS server handles the entire query process and returns the final result directly. For example: The client submits "Please find the IP of www.example.com" to the local DNS (recursive server). If the recursive server does not know the answer, it will continuously query the root DNS, TLD, and authoritative DNS, following each step until it obtains the final answer. The user only needs to wait for the recursive server to provide the final resolution result. Characteristics The user only needs to ask once and wait once. The recursive server bears higher load because it completes the entire query. This is the most common method between clients and local DNS. Common applications Regular users/PCs/browsers: Only use recursive queries and do not process iterative queries. Local DNS servers (such as your broadband carrier, public 8.8.8.8, or company intranet DNS): Usually take on the role of recursive resolution and initiate iterative queries externally (to root, TLD, authoritative DNS). Public DNS servers: Recursive for users, iterative for higher-level DNS. Iterative resolution (Iterative Query) The DNS server only provides the next referral it knows. If it lacks the answer, it tells you where to ask next, and you query each step yourself. For example: The client asks the local DNS: "What is the IP of www.example.com?" The local DNS does not know and queries the root DNS. The root DNS does not directly answer with the IP but responds: "You should ask the TLD server." Here, the root DNS is performing iterative resolution. The local DNS asks the TLD server, and the TLD tells you "go find this authoritative DNS." Here, the TLD server is performing iterative resolution. This continues until the authoritative server is found and the final result is obtained. Characteristics The client needs to ask at each step. The server load is relatively low, but the client burden is high (not typically used this way). Common applications Root DNS servers, TLD servers, authoritative DNS servers. Self-built DNS servers for enterprises, schools, etc. Public DNS servers: Recursive for users, iterative for higher-level DNS. DNS cache A DNS cache stores resolution data geographically close to the requesting client. Caching can occur at any level in the query hierarchy to reduce latency and recursive query volume. TTL Time to Live (TTL) is a DNS record value that specifies how long a recursive resolver caches the record. After expiration, the resolver discards the cached record and performs a fresh query on the next request. To configure TTL values for your DNS records, follow How to configure TTL. IPv4/IPv6 dual stack A dual IP stack, or dual stack, allows a system to run both IPv4 and IPv6 protocol stacks simultaneously in parallel. DNS query flood attack A DNS query flood attack is a DoS attack in which compromised machines send massive volumes of queries to a target DNS server. When queries per second exceed server capacity, legitimate requests may time out, impacting service availability. URL forwarding URL forwarding (URL redirection) uses server-side settings to redirect requests from one domain to another website. edns-client-subnet edns-client-subnet is a DNS extension protocol that allows recursive resolvers to pass client IP information to authoritative DNS servers. HTTPDNS mobile resolution concepts Application terminal Terminal devices and applications that access network services, such as mobile devices, IoT devices, and apps. DNS over HTTPS (DoH) Encrypts DNS traffic over TLS-encrypted HTTP connections (RFC 8484). Supported by Alibaba Cloud Public DNS. DNS over TLS (DoT) Encrypts DNS traffic over TLS-encrypted TCP connections (RFC 7858). Supported by Alibaba Cloud Public DNS. Previous: What is Alibaba Cloud DNS Next: Quick links Is this page helpful? Source: Architectural Analysis of the Modern Web Request Lifecycle
Type: Pasted Text
Architectural Analysis of the Modern Web Request LifecycleClient Initiation and URI ProcessingThe initiation of a modern web request is governed by the parsing of a Uniform Resource Identifier (URI) in strict compliance with RFC 3986.[1] When a user enters a domain name into a client application or clicks a link, the browser’s request initiation process begins with a local parsing sequence to break down the scheme, host, port, path, and query parameters.[1, 2, 3] Before a single packet is dispatched to the network, the client must evaluate the target host against its local security policies.[1, 4]Under RFC 6797, the browser checks if the domain matches any host in its local HTTP Strict Transport Security (HSTS) cache.[1, 4] If a valid match is found, or if the domain is a subdomain of a host that specified the includeSubDomains directive, the browser performs an internal, client-side protocol upgrade.[1, 4] It replaces the unencrypted http:// scheme with https:// and redirects the target port from 80 to 443.[1, 3]To protect users against protocol downgrade and cookie hijacking attacks on their very first visit to a site, browsers query a hardcoded HSTS Preload list.[4, 5] This list, maintained primarily by the Chromium project but integrated across all major browsers, contains domains that have pre-committed to exclusive HTTPS delivery.[3, 4, 5] If the domain is present in this built-in database, the client forbids any plaintext fallback connections, ensuring that the initial request is transmitted over an encrypted channel.[3, 4]The Domain Name System: State Management and Hierarchy ResolutionTo map the logical host name parsed from the URI to a routable IP address, the client invokes its local stub resolver.[6, 7] The Domain Name System (DNS) historically operates over the User Datagram Protocol (UDP) on Port 53, optimizing for speed and minimizing connection-establishment overhead.[8, 9] While UDP is fundamentally a connectionless, stateless transport, recursive resolvers are highly stateful machines pretending to be stateless services.[9] Under high load, every cache miss forces a recursive resolver to allocate a pending-query slot in its memory tables while it awaits upstream authoritative answers.[9]This structural pattern exposes a significant vulnerability: because the resolver does not control the incoming questions, malicious actors can flood it with unique, un-cacheable random subdomain queries.[9] This tactic exhausts the resolver’s pending-query slots, rendering it incapable of serving legitimate queries—a vector exploited by the NXNS attack which leverages name server records lacking glue IP addresses to amplify query volumes.[9] To secure these endpoints, resolvers require the deployment of DNS Cookies under RFC 7873.[9]Traditional UDP DNS responses are limited to a legacy ceiling of 512 bytes.[9] Modern extensions, specifically Extension Mechanisms for DNS (EDNS0), permit resolvers to advertise larger buffer sizes, typically stabilizing at a safe ceiling of 1232 bytes to avoid IPv4/IPv6 packet fragmentation across intermediate links.[9] When cryptographic DNSSEC chains, AXFR zone transfers, or expansive TXT records push the payload beyond this limit, the authoritative server returns a truncated response with the TC (Truncation) bit set.[9] This forces the resolver to discard the UDP packet and fall back to TCP Port 53.[9]Operating DNS over TCP introduces standard TCP vulnerabilities.[9] Attackers can target TCP/53 using Sockstress (completing handshakes and advertising a window size of 0 to lock up resolver process file descriptors) or Slowloris (sending the 2-byte query length prefix and stalling transmission of subsequent bytes to keep TCP idle timeouts from firing).[9] Similarly, DNS-over-TLS (DoT) on Port 853 exposes resolvers to incomplete TLS ClientHello floods and handshake exhaustion.[9]When a local DNS cache miss occurs, the recursive resolver executes an iterative resolution process across the DNS hierarchy.[6, 8] The resolver queries a pre-configured Root Nameserver (.), which returns an iterative referral pointing to the Top-Level Domain (TLD) nameservers.[8] The resolver then hops to the TLD nameserver (e.g., the .com registry), receiving another referral to the Authoritative Nameservers delegated for the specific domain zone.[8] The resolver sends its final query to the authoritative server, which returns the mapping.[8, 10]For validating forwarding resolvers operating on high-latency links, the EDNS0 CHAIN query extension under RFC 7901 permits the forwarder to open a TCP connection to an upstream recursive resolver and request a complete validation path in a single query/answer flight, reducing the multiple round trips of traditional iterative lookups down to two.[6]To secure this transit, recursive resolvers perform DNSSEC validation.[10] Using a pre-installed public trust anchor (typically for the root zone), the resolver cryptographically decrypts the digital signatures contained in RRSIG records using public keys published in DNSKEY records.[10] By hashing these keys and comparing them to Delegation Signer (DS) records provided by parent zones, the resolver verifies the cryptographic chain of trust down to the child leaf zone.[10] If the computed hash fails to match the signature, the resolver discards the data and returns a SERVFAIL message to the client, preventing on-path spoofing and cache poisoning.[10]The duration for which resolvers cache these records is governed by the Time-To-Live (TTL) value.[7] The TTL defines a hard ceiling for record reuse, after which the resolver must execute a fresh query.[7, 11] Under RFC 2308, negative caching—the practice of remembering that a domain or resource record does not exist to prevent redundant queries—is controlled by the Minimum field in the Start of Authority (SOA) record.[7, 11] The effective duration for negative caching is calculated as:\text{Effective Negative TTL} = \min(\text{SOA TTL}, \ \text{SOA Minimum}) [11]Transport Layer Mechanics: TCP State Machine and Kernel AllocationWith the IP address resolved, the client initiates a reliable transport channel by executing the Transmission Control Protocol (TCP) three-way handshake.[12] This protocol relies on Positive Acknowledgment with Retransmission (PAR) and checksum validation to guarantee ordered, error-free delivery of byte streams.[12] The handshake progresses through a strict series of kernel-managed states:The client begins with an active open, dispatching a SYN segment and transitioning to the SYN-SENT state.[13, 14, 15] The client generates a cryptographically random 32-bit Initial Sequence Number (ISN) to defend against sequence number prediction and session injection attacks.[16] The server, waiting in the LISTEN state, receives the SYN, allocates a Transmission Control Block (TCB) in memory, and transitions to SYN-RECEIVED.[13, 14, 15] It returns a SYN-ACK containing its own random ISN (Sequence Y) and sets the Acknowledgment number to X + 1.[12, 17] The client processes this response, transitions to ESTABLISHED, and returns its final ACK (Sequence X + 1, Acknowledgment Y + 1).[13, 14, 17] Upon receipt, the server transitions to ESTABLISHED, opening the socket for application-layer payloads.[13, 14, 15]To locate active sockets upon packet arrival, the host kernel maintains a global TCP established hash table.[15] The size of this table is determined during system boot based on total memory allocation.[15] A typical system with 8 GB of RAM may instantiate 524,288 established hash table entries.[15] When a socket is actively closed, it transitions through FIN-WAIT-1 and FIN-WAIT-2 states before resting in the TIME-WAIT state for twice the Maximum Segment Lifetime (2\text{MSL}), which ensures that delayed segments from the closed connection are not accepted by subsequent connections reusing the same address and port quadruplet.[13, 15]To prevent memory exhaustion from millions of dormant connections, the Linux kernel divides its allocation footprints.[15] An active socket is allocated via struct tcp_sock (requiring ~1776 bytes).[15] A socket in TIME-WAIT is downgraded to struct tcp_timewait_sock (requiring only ~168 bytes) and managed via a dedicated "death row" structure.[15] This death row, implemented as a hash table since Linux 4.1, allows efficient parallel execution and connection expiration without locking up active thread queues.[15] Outbound dynamic binds are tracked via a dedicated port allocation table composed of struct inet_bind_socket elements (~48 bytes each) to determine if a local port is safe for reuse.[15]Congestion Control and Flow ManagementData flow over an active TCP connection is bounded by the minimum of the receiver's advertised window (rwnd), which prevents buffer overflow at the destination host, and the sender's congestion window (cwnd), which prevents network saturation.[18, 19, 20] The volume of in-flight, unacknowledged data is bounded as:\text{FlightSize} \leq \min(cwnd, \ rwnd) [18, 21]Under RFC 6928, modern TCP stacks initialize the congestion window (IW) to 10 Maximum Segment Sizes (MSS), typically equivalent to 14,600 bytes.[22, 23] This initial window of 10 segments reduces the total transfer time for standard web objects (often exceeding 4 KB) by up to four round trips compared to legacy limits.[22]The lifecycle of the cwnd is managed across two primary phases: Slow Start and Congestion Avoidance.[18, 19, 20] During Slow Start, which operates while cwnd is below the slow-start threshold (ssthresh), the sender exponentially probes network capacity, incrementing cwnd by 1\text{ MSS} for every received cumulative ACK:cwnd_{t+1} = cwnd_t + MSS [19, 20]This doubles the window size with each round-trip time (RTT).[12, 20] Once cwnd reaches ssthresh, the sender transitions to Congestion Avoidance to scale linearly, avoiding network saturation:cwnd_{t+1} = cwnd_t + \frac{MSS^2}{cwnd_t} [19, 20]This grows the window by approximately 1\text{ MSS} per RTT.[18, 19, 20] If segment loss is detected, the stack must adjust ssthresh.[18, 20] When a loss is signaled via a Retransmission Timeout (RTO), ssthresh is calculated as:ssthresh = \max\left(\frac{1}{2} \times \text{FlightSize}, \ 2 \times MSS\right) [20, 21]The transition logic diverges across congestion control engine profiles:TCP Tahoe: On any loss detection, the engine drops the active cwnd back to 1\text{ MSS}, forcing a return to Slow Start.[19, 20]TCP Reno: On the receipt of three duplicate ACKs, the engine executes Fast Retransmit, sets ssthresh and cwnd to half of the current cwnd, and enters Fast Recovery to continue Congestion Avoidance without restarting from Slow Start.[19, 20]TCP CUBIC: The default engine in modern Linux kernels. Instead of linear AIMD increases, CUBIC utilizes a cubic growth function based on the time elapsed since the last loss event.[19, 23] It registers the maximum window size (W_{max}) prior to a loss.[19] After a window reduction, it scales growth rapidly in a concave profile, decelerates as it approaches W_{max} to allow the network to stabilize, and then accelerates in a convex profile to probe for new capacity.[19]QUIC Congestion Adapters: Modern QUIC implementations run hybrid slow-start algorithms that continuously monitor RTT variations to distinguish between actual network congestion and transient queuing delay, preventing sudden, severe throughput drops.[24]Cryptographic Session Security: TLS Handshake EvolutionWith a stable transport link established, the application layer secures its payload using the Transport Layer Security (TLS) handshake.[2, 27] The transition from TLS 1.2 to TLS 1.3 represents a significant optimization in speed and security.[27, 28]The core architectural optimization in TLS 1.3 is the reduction of the handshake to a single round trip (1 RTT).[27, 28, 29] In the ClientHello flight, the client sends its supported TLS versions, compatible cipher suites, and a client-generated random nonce.[2, 27, 29] Crucially, it speculatively generates and transmits ephemeral public key shares using Elliptic Curve Diffie-Hellman (ECDHE) algorithms.[2, 29, 30] The server responds with its ServerHello, selecting the cipher suite and returning its own ECDHE public key share alongside its digital certificate.[2, 27, 29] Because all handshake elements after the ServerHello are encrypted, eavesdroppers cannot determine which specific host certificate is being negotiated.[2, 30]TLS 1.3 leverages the "Locked Box Trick"—a sequence combining asymmetric and symmetric cryptography.[29] Asymmetric operations are mathematically intensive and run 100 to 1000 times slower than symmetric operations.[29] Consequently, TLS 1.3 uses asymmetric cryptography (ECDHE) solely to negotiate a shared secret over an unencrypted channel without transmitting the actual key material.[27, 29, 30]Once this secret is established, both parties derive symmetric session keys to encrypt bulk application data using fast, hardware-accelerated algorithms like AES-GCM or ChaCha20-Poly1305, which combine payload encryption and message authentication (AEAD).[28, 29] By mandating ECDHE, TLS 1.3 eliminates static RSA key transport.[27] This enforces Perfect Forward Secrecy (PFS), guaranteeing that even if a server's private key is compromised in the future, past recorded sessions cannot be decrypted because the ephemeral session keys are long gone.[27, 28, 30]In TLS 1.2, the target hostname is exposed in plaintext via the Server Name Indication (SNI) extension inside the ClientHello.[2] TLS 1.3 mitigates this exposure through Encrypted Client Hello (ECH).[2] ECH splits the client hello into an inner, encrypted portion containing the actual target host (e.g., mybank.com) and an outer, plaintext portion containing a non-sensitive public facing host (e.g., a CDN distributor).[2] Only the designated ECH-compatible server or load balancer can decrypt the inner payload, shielding the target domain from network snooping.[2]To verify the server’s identity, the client validates the digital certificate through a hierarchical Chain of Trust.[2, 27, 29] The validation steps proceed as follows:Leaf Certificate Check: The browser evaluates the server's leaf certificate, confirming that the requested domain matches the Subject Alternative Name (SAN), that the validity period is current, and that the certificate has not been revoked (via CRL or OCSP queries).[27, 30]Intermediate CA Signature Verification: The leaf certificate is signed with the private key of an Intermediate CA.[2, 29] The browser uses the public key from the Intermediate CA certificate to verify the cryptographic signature on the leaf.[2, 29]Root Anchor Mapping: The Intermediate CA certificate must be signed by a Root CA.[2, 29] The browser traces the chain to the self-signed Root CA certificate and verifies its presence in the local trust store.[2, 27, 29] If the Root CA is trusted and every signature in the chain is valid, the secure session is opened.[29] If any link is expired or untrusted, the browser aborts the handshake.[29]Application Layer Protocols: HTTP Architectural ParadigmsUpon establishing a secure transport layer, the application layer coordinates resource delivery.[31, 32] The architectural evolution of HTTP from HTTP/1.1 to HTTP/3 focuses on optimizing concurrency on increasingly lossy and complex network paths.[31, 33]HTTP/1.1: Standardized in 1997/1999 under RFC 2068/2616, it operates over TCP.[33, 34] While it introduced persistent connections, it suffers from application-layer Head-of-Line Blocking (HOLB).[31, 33] Requests on a single connection must be processed sequentially; if a resource is slow to generate, all subsequent assets in the pipeline are stalled.[33, 35] To bypass this, browsers open up to 6–8 parallel TCP connections per origin, multiplying transport handshakes and network congestion.[33] Furthermore, HTTP/1.1 transmits headers as verbose, plain ASCII text, incurring high data overhead.[33]HTTP/2: Standardized in 2015 under RFC 7540, it introduced binary framing and stream multiplexing over a single TCP connection.[31, 33, 36] By breaking HTTP requests and responses into discrete binary frames, HTTP/2 allows multiple bidirectional streams to be interleaved on a single connection.[33, 36] Client-initiated streams utilize odd numeric identifiers to maintain isolation.[33] Header redundancy is minimized using HPACK compression.[36] However, because HTTP/2 runs over TCP, it remains vulnerable to transport-layer HOLB.[31, 33] If a single TCP packet is dropped, the host kernel halts processing of all multiplexed streams until the lost segment is retransmitted, degrading performance on lossy networks.[24, 31, 33]HTTP/3: Standardized in 2022 under RFC 9114, it replaces the underlying TCP layer with the QUIC transport protocol, which runs over UDP.[31, 33, 37] QUIC shifts connection reliability, packet sequencing, and flow control into user space.[24, 25] By embedding independent multiplexed streams directly into the transport layer, QUIC achieves complete stream isolation.[26, 31] If a packet belonging to Stream A is lost, only Stream A is paused while awaiting retransmission.[26, 31] Streams B and C continue uninterrupted, eliminating transport-layer HOLB.[26, 31]Additionally, QUIC leverages stable, unique Connection IDs (CIDs) rather than the client's transient IP address and port.[26] When a client migrates between physical networks (such as transitioning from Wi-Fi to cellular data), it executes path probing.[26] Once the new path is validated, the session is shifted using the same CID.[26] Active streams, cryptographic keys, and congestion states are preserved without initiating a new handshake.[26]Despite the performance advantages of HTTP/3, global adoption data reveals a steady plateau.[34] Real-world metrics indicate that while HTTP/2 remains the dominant protocol (~51%), HTTP/3 usage has plateaued at approximately 21.2%.[34]This plateau is driven by several factors.[33, 34, 37] Approximately 3% to 5% of corporate networks and enterprise firewalls systematically block all outbound UDP traffic, forcing clients to fall back to TCP-based HTTP/2.[33, 37] Furthermore, because QUIC is implemented in user space to allow rapid protocol iteration without requiring OS kernel modifications, it incurs significant context-switching overhead.[24, 33] Under high-throughput conditions (exceeding 500 Mbps), user-space scheduling limitations can cause up to a 45.2% reduction in performance compared to kernel-optimized TCP.[24, 34]Dynamic Network Routing: Gateway Traversal and Path SelectionFor application packets to reach the host destination, they must navigate the routing infrastructure of the internet.[38] When the client operating system constructs an IP packet, it checks its local routing table to determine if the destination IP falls within the local subnet.[38] If the destination is external, the packet is directed to the default gateway.[39, 40]To frame the Layer 3 IP packet for Layer 2 transit, the host must resolve the default gateway's IP to a physical MAC address.[39] It executes an Address Resolution Protocol (ARP) request for IPv4, or Neighbor Discovery Protocol (NDP) request for IPv6, caching the gateway's MAC address to wrap the IP packet inside an Ethernet frame.[38, 39]In modern scalable datacenters, local Layer 2 domains are kept small to isolate fault boundaries.[38] Top-of-Rack (ToR) switch pairs act as Layer 3 gateways, routing host-to-host traffic via VXLAN overlays using Ethernet VPN (EVPN) with Integrated Routing and Bridging (IRB).[38, 39] Under the EVPN IRB model, Layer 3 routing instances configure a Bridge Domain Virtual Interface (BVI) per broadcast domain.[39, 41] EVPN PE switches learn local MAC and IP bindings via ARP-snooping and propagate them across the leaf-spine fabric using EVPN Type-2 (MAC/IP) or Type-5 (IP Prefix) route updates, utilizing Generalized Learning (G-LEAN) adjacencies when paths are unresolved.[38, 39]To ensure high availability at the network edge, gateways operate in distinct modes [40]:Layer 3 Mode: Provides stateful security for through-traffic without directly connected Layer 2 resources.[40]Default Gateway Mode: Connects Layer 2 resources via a Virtual IP (VIP) using First Hop Redundancy Protocols like VRRP.[40]Hybrid Mode: Combines Layer 3 and Default Gateway modes, dynamically influencing traffic flow based on which node actively manages the VIP.[40]Edge routers establish BGP peering sessions over physical or logical interfaces, operating either as single-hop sessions for directly connected peers or multi-hop sessions for remote peers.[40] To maintain path availability during uplink failures, ToR switches configure backup routing paths over virtual Port Channel (vPC) peer-links.[42] This is achieved either by defining transit VLAN SVIs as passive interfaces to prevent routing adjacencies over the peer-link or by establishing dedicated point-to-point Layer 3 backup links.[42]To push Layer 3 routing further down the stack and isolate tenant domains, modern systems turn hypervisors into L3 routers.[38] Using agents like Felix (from the Calico project), hypervisors rely on Netfilter to isolate administrative domains.[38] The hypervisor configures a /32 route with the virtual interface of the guest as the next hop and distributes it via BGP, keeping guest VMs blind to the change while allowing them to retrieve configurations via DHCP.[38] To prevent packet leaks, hypervisors build copy routing tables (such as local-out tables) that shadow public and private routes with high-metric default paths.[38]Once traffic exits the local autonomous system, routing is governed by the Border Gateway Protocol (BGP).[43, 44] BGP path attributes are categorized into four structural domains:Well-Known Mandatory: Must be recognized by all BGP-compliant routers and included in every BGP Update message (e.g., Origin, AS_PATH, and Next_Hop).[45]Well-Known Discretionary: Must be recognized by all routers but is optional in BGP updates (e.g., LOCAL_PREF).[45]Optional Transitive: May not be recognized by all routers, but unrecognized routers must forward the attribute to their next neighbor (e.g., Community).[45]Optional Non-Transitive: May be ignored if unrecognized, and must not be forwarded to neighboring routers (e.g., Cluster List).[45]When a router receives multiple path advertisements for a single prefix, it evaluates them against the BGP path selection decision hierarchy [45, 46, 47]:Server-Side Computing and Processing RuntimesWhen the routing layer delivers the packet to the destination server, the request is intercepted by the web server runtime.[32, 48] The scalability of this tier is heavily dependent on the runtime's memory allocation and CPU scheduling models.[49] Production infrastructures evaluate three core concurrency models: JVM Platform Threads, JVM Virtual Threads (Project Loom), and the Node.js Single-Threaded Event Loop.[49]JVM Platform Threads: Every active request maps directly to a 1:1 Operating System (OS) thread.[49] Each thread pre-allocates approximately 1 MB of stack memory.[49] This model handles parallel CPU operations efficiently by utilizing OS-level multi-threading.[49] However, for I/O-bound web requests, these threads spend substantial time blocked while awaiting database or API responses.[48, 49] This limits scalability, as a system attempting to scale to 10,000 concurrent sockets consumes 10 GB of RAM just to maintain empty thread stacks, running into the C10K memory boundary.[49]JVM Virtual Threads (Project Loom): Introduces an M:N scheduling abstraction.[49] Millions of lightweight virtual threads are managed entirely by the JVM, demanding only 200 to 300 bytes of metadata per instance.[49] When a virtual thread encounters a blocking I/O operation, the JVM scheduler unmounts the virtual thread from its underlying OS carrier thread.[49] The carrier thread remains free to execute other tasks, while the virtual thread waits in JVM memory.[49] Once the I/O operation completes, the scheduler mounts the virtual thread back onto any available carrier thread to resume execution.[49] This preserves the synchronous coding model while reducing memory footprint and context-switching overhead.[49]Node.js Single-Threaded Event Loop: JavaScript execution runs on a single thread, achieving concurrency by offloading I/O operations to the multi-threaded system kernel or the internal libuv thread pool, keeping the main execution thread free.[49, 50] The event loop schedules execution across microtask queues (which hold priority and process process.nextTick and Promises) and macrotask queues (which manage timers and I/O callbacks).[49] This model is memory-efficient, demanding only ~1.2 GB of RAM for 50,000 idle connections.[49]However, because JavaScript execution is single-threaded, any CPU-intensive task blocks the main execution thread.[48, 49] When blocked, the event loop cannot advance to the polling phase, starving all concurrent I/O operations and degrading request processing.[48, 49]To bridge the gap between traditional multithreading and reactive non-blocking execution, the Java Servlet API supports asynchronous processing.[48] By invoking request.startAsync(), a servlet container thread can release its execution thread back to the main pool while waiting for slow database operations, allowing a smaller pool of platform threads to handle higher request volumes without resource exhaustion.[48]Output Rendering Models: Delivery and SEO OptimizationOnce the runtime processes the request, the application layer generates and delivers the output payload.[32, 51] Modern architectures evaluate three primary rendering models, each balancing client performance, server load, and search engine optimization (SEO) [32, 35, 51]:Server-Side Rendering (SSR): The server queries databases, executes business logic, and compiles a complete, static HTML document.[51, 52] This HTML is delivered to the client as a stream of data chunks.[35, 51] SSR optimizes initial load times and compatibility, but requires a client-side "hydration" process to attach JavaScript event listeners to the static DOM.[51] To minimize hydration overhead, modern systems use "islands" architecture, shipping smaller JavaScript chunks to isolate interactivity to designated sections of the page.[51]Client-Side Rendering (CSR): The server returns a minimal HTML shell and a large JavaScript bundle.[35, 51] The client browser downloads and executes this bundle, making asynchronous API requests to fetch JSON data and construct the DOM dynamically.[35, 51] While CSR reduces server CPU load, it delays initial rendering on low-power devices.[51]Backend-Driven Frontend: The backend generates and transmits a structured JSON schema defining both layout configurations (such as component types, alignment, typography, and styling) and raw data.[32] The client-side application parses this schema at runtime and dynamically instantiates native UI elements.[32] This decouples presentation from client-side compilation, allowing instantaneous interface updates across multiple platforms without requiring client application updates.[32]For CSR-heavy Single Page Applications (SPAs) that require robust search indexation, developers deploy headless rendering engines like Renderron.[53] Renderron runs an Express server wrapping a headless Chrome instance to pre-render JavaScript-heavy pages into static HTML for web crawler user agents.[53] To bypass the startup latency of launching headless browsers dynamically, production systems implement caching layers alongside proactive cache-warming loops, pre-rendering and caching paths periodically to ensure search crawlers receive responses immediately without triggering timeout failures.[53]

Try it free BGP MONITORING BGP Path Selection BGP doesn't just pick the fastest path, it follows a strict attribute evaluation order to select the best policy-based route. Learn the full decision process and how to control it. 10–15 minutes April 5, 2026 Denton Chikura IN THIS DEEP DIVE Executive Summary BGP Path Selection BGP Defaults Prefix Length Administrative Policy BGP Attributes Typical Use Cases RPKI ROA Considerations Conclusion The quick download: BGP path selection follows a strict attribute evaluation order, if you don't understand the sequence, you can't reliably predict which path your router will choose, or debug it when it chooses wrong. LOCAL_PREF is evaluated first (among changeable attributes), making it the most powerful lever for outbound traffic engineering within an AS. AS_PATH length is one of the most commonly manipulated attributes, prepending your own AS adds length to make a path less preferred. IGP metric to NEXT_HOP is evaluated late in the process but can be the deciding factor when all other attributes are equal. Document your intended path selection behavior at every peering boundary, then validate it with actual traffic flow analysis, BGP will follow its algorithm precisely, which may not always match your assumptions. Many activities essential to modern life depend on the internet, and the internet depends on the Border GaIn this article, we will look at how the BGP protocol chooses the best route to use for forwarding traffic. With other routing protocols, discriminating between paths and selecting the best ones will help ensure optimal network utilization and great performance. Other protocols, such as OSPF or Spanning Tree protocols, optimize for link state, minimal latency, and using the fastest path first. In BGP, however, the emphasis is on policy-based path selection. BGP optimizes for shorter paths at the autonomous system level rather than the link level, and it optimizes for best path according to the relationship with the neighboring autonomous system. BGP is not like a true link-state or distance-vector protocol — it is known as a path vector protocol. It was designed from the ground up to offer fine-grained administrative policy control over path selection. Baked into the BGP protocol and its implementations are sensible defaults ensuring reasonably optimal path selection even without a policy being applied. Most implementations will, by default, select a single best path to a given IP destination. We will use Juniper JunOS examples across this article to show you how the administrator can influence BGP path selection. We'll cover some implementation details and typical use cases with policy examples. Note that we will focus on path selection between BGP routes installed in the routing table; out of scope will be BGP routes that are filtered out by policy or deemed invalid due to, for example, AS path loops. We also won't cover vendor-specific path selection features, such as Cisco's “weight” attribute, which is not part of the BGP standard. Executive Summary We will explain the main factors influencing BGP path selection, which are the following. BGP Path Selection BGP Defaults Each router vendor implements its own BGP path selection defaults. Notably, Cisco includes a vendor-specific parameter called Weight. The path with the highest weight is always chosen. As mentioned, in this article, we will use Juniper Junos for our explanation and examples. Juniper documents its default selection process on this page, where you can read about the selection process and how various tiebreakers are evaluated. It's essential to note that routes are compared and selected at each step of the algorithm using sorting and tiebreaking. For example, let's say we start with two eBGP routes to the same destination, and one has a higher BGP local preference than the other. At step two of the process, the route with the higher local preference will be chosen, and the process stops (for that destination). If, instead, these two routes had the same local preference, but one had a shorter AS path than the other, it would be chosen at step five of the process. Quoting from Juniper's article, and simplifying some details, the full algorithm looks essentially like this: Verify that the next hop can be resolved. Choose the path with the lowest preference value (routing protocol process preference). For example, static routes are preferred over IGP routes, IGP routes are preferred over BGP routes, and so on. Prefer the path with higher local preference. If the accumulated interior gateway protocol (AIGP) attribute is enabled, prefer the path with the lower AIGP attribute. That is, for routes internal to your BGP domain, prefer the route with the lowest IGP metric or cost. Prefer the path with the shortest autonomous system (AS) path value (skipped if the as-path-ignore statement is configured). A confederation segment (sequence or set) has a path length of 0. An AS set has a path length of 1. Prefer the route with the lower origin code. Routes learned from an IGP have a lower origin code than those learned from an exterior gateway protocol (EGP), and both have lower origin codes than incomplete routes (those whose origin is unknown). Prefer the path with the lowest multiple exit discriminator (MED) metric. Confederations are not considered when determining neighboring ASes. A missing MED metric is treated as if a MED were present but zero. By default, only the MEDs of routes that have the same peer autonomous systems (ASes) are compared. Prefer strictly internal paths, which include IGP routes and locally generated routes (static, direct, local, and so forth). Prefer strictly external BGP (EBGP) paths over external paths learned through internal BGP (IBGP) sessions. Prefer the path whose next hop is resolved through the IGP route with the lowest metric. NOTE: A path is considered a BGP equal-cost path (and will be used for forwarding) if a tiebreak is performed after the previous step. All paths with the same neighboring AS, learned by a multipath-enabled BGP neighbor, are considered. BGP multipath does not apply to paths that share the same MED-plus-IGP cost yet differ in IGP cost. Multipath path selection is based on the IGP cost metric, even if two paths have the same MED-plus-IGP cost. If both paths are external, prefer the currently active path to minimize route-flapping. This rule is not used if any one of the following conditions is true: path-selection external-router-id is configured. Both peers have the same router ID. Either peer is a confederation peer. Neither path is the current active path. Prefer a primary route over a secondary route. A primary route is one that belongs to the routing table, while a secondary route is added to the routing table through an export policy. Prefer the path from the peer with the lowest router ID. For any path with an originator ID attribute, substitute the originator ID for the router ID during router ID comparison. Prefer the path with the shortest cluster list length. The length is 0 for no list. Prefer the path from the peer with the lowest peer IP address. Prefix Length The algorithm in the previous section explains how the best route is selected from a set of routes with the same prefix length, but it's crucial to note that the most specific prefix, i.e., the longest subnet mask, will always be chosen first. Let's consider some examples: with the RPKI, these technologies introduce mechanisms that help mitigate hijacking and malicious attacks on your internet routing. Routes A and D will be selected as active routes for their given prefix lengths, but a selection process will occur between routes B and C because they have the same prefix length. Route A covers the range 192.168.0.0 through 192.168.255.255. Route D covers the range 192.168.1.128 through 192.168.1.255. Routes B and C cover the range 192.168.1.0 through 192.168.1.255. Let's consider the implications of the different prefix lengths when a packet forwarding decision is made: When a packet is forwarded to 192.168.1.120, route B or C will be chosen. When a packet is forwarded to 192.168.1.130, route D will be chosen, because it has a longer prefix. When a packet is forwarded to anything inside the range 192.168.0.0 – 192.168.255.255 that does not match the more specific /25 and /24 routes, route A will be chosen. Administrative Policy Let's move on to policy. As mentioned, BGP offers fine-grained policy controls for path selection and the specific ordering in the algorithm is intended to give sensible results, and often near-optimal ones, even without a specific policy being applied. However, it is often the case that a given BGP peering should be preferred over another for reasons of bandwidth cost per Mbps, or latency, or relationship to that peer. BGP itself is not aware of peer relationships, path latency, link or path bandwidth, or the financial cost of that bandwidth. This is where you, as the administrator, come in. A very typical arrangement is to have a tiered local preference structure based on relationship, for example: Customer routes are most preferred, with a higher-than-default local preference, e.g., 110. Private or internet exchange routes are preferred next, with a default local preference, e.g., 100. Transit routes are the least preferred, with a lower-than-default preference, e.g., 90. A tiered local preference structure like this is used because the best path is always the most direct one. You wouldn't route traffic out of your network to take a scenic route via the internet transit providers to reach your customer. Accordingly, you want to always prefer your direct links to customers to reach their prefixes, and direct or exchange links to peers to reach peers, and then use transit for everything else. On the financial side, customers pay you, peers exchange traffic settlement-free, generally speaking, and you pay your transit providers. BGP Attributes The main attributes worthy of consideration when creating policy and understanding how route selection will work in operation are the following: Local preference AS path length MED (metric) Note: As described earlier, prefix length is not a BGP attribute but supersedes BGP attribute route selection criteria. When crafting policy adjustments to local preference or MED, also consider how AS path length plays a role and how route selection affects how traffic will route and reroute as links fail, routers go down for maintenance, etc. Typical Use Cases Let's get a little more granular with the local preference policy outlined previously. If we add some distinctions, such as offering customers primary and backup connections, we might set the primary to local preference 110 and the backup to local preference 105. This ensures that no matter the value of any other route attributes, primary connections remain primary and active if available, and backup connections remain as idle backups, only becoming active if no primary is available. With peering connections, we might differentiate between directly connected peers, which cross-connect directly to our router in the data center, and indirectly connected peers, which meet at an internet exchange point such as a shared LAN switch. If we had two transit providers and one was more expensive than the other but otherwise similarly performing, we might elect to use local preference 90 for the cheaper transit and local preference 85 for the more expensive one. Again, this would ensure that the cheaper transit is used when available, and the expensive transit is only used when the primary is down. Our local preference schema would then look like this: RPKI ROA Considerations Harking back to the introduction, which explains that filtered or otherwise invalid routes are out of scope for route selection, it's important to note that in a global network of BGP sessions, RPKI ROA policies play an important part in route selection. This article primarily outlines how you, as an administrator, can influence and manage route selection in your network. However, be aware that networks implementing ROA validation policies use RPKI ROA attributes to determine whether to accept a route advertisement or reject it, and if accepting it, whether to use the standard or a lowered local preference value. A quick summary is that every AS number on the internet has the option to create ROA records detailing which origin AS numbers and prefix lengths are valid for its IP space. Networks evaluating ROAs as part of their BGP import policies will check to see if a route they receive has RPKI ROA status valid, invalid, or unknown. ROAs look like this (simplified): This means any BGP route seen on the internet for the IP range 192.168.0.0 – 192.168.1.255 must originate from AS65000 and must be in the form of a route prefix 192.168.0.0/23, 192.168.0.0/24, or 192.168.1.0/24. Any other route referencing this IP range will be deemed invalid because it clashes with this ROA specification. Therefore, a valid route is one that matches a ROA record, while an unknown route is one that has no ROA covering the IP space to which it pertains. Most networks accept valid and unknown routes because, at the time of writing, only one-quarter to one-third of the internet routing table is covered by ROAs. If the route is valid, it will be accepted, and if the route has no ROA, the same—the alternative is to drop connectivity to most of the internet! RPKI ROA adoption is growing, but it will be some time before most internet prefixes are covered. Until then, the only sane policy is to accept unknowns. As an newtork operator or engineer, it's important for you to know that invalid routes are those that clash with a ROA. Many large transit providers now drop invalid routes; some will accept them, but with a lower local preference value. The RPKI ROA system is designed to prevent misconfigurations in route announcements, with the key implication being that it helps prevent BGP route hijacking. The takeaway is that if you or your customers create ROAs for your prefixes, you absolutely must take great care to maintain accuracy. Otherwise, you may find yourself cut off from the internet as your BGP announcements are dropped! Conclusion You should now understand the basic considerations for default path selection, how you can change this behavior using policy, and why you might want to do so. We also touched on RPKI ROA considerations, including the benefits and risks of deploying them. Equipped with this knowledge, you are hopefully now empowered to begin traffic engineering your own BGP sessions for best effect. Validate your routing policy is working as intended. LogicMonitor offers network teams visibility into active paths, routing table changes, and BGP attribute states so you can confirm your traffic engineering policies are producing the outcomes you designed. Schedule a demo FAQs How does BGP path selection work? BGP path selection is a sequential comparison of route attributes. When multiple paths exist to the same destination, BGP evaluates them in order: highest WEIGHT (Cisco-specific), highest LOCAL_PREF, locally originated routes, shortest AS_PATH, lowest ORIGIN code, lowest MED (when comparable), eBGP over iBGP, lowest IGP metric to NEXT_HOP, and finally tiebreakers like oldest eBGP route and lowest router ID. The first attribute that produces a winner ends the comparison. What is the most important BGP attribute for traffic engineering? For outbound traffic engineering (influencing which path your AS uses to reach other networks), LOCAL_PREF is the most powerful attribute because it's evaluated early and shared across all iBGP peers within the AS. For inbound traffic engineering (influencing how other ASes route traffic to you), AS_PATH prepending is the most widely supported technique, though MED can also be used when agreed upon with a neighboring AS. Why does BGP prefer eBGP routes over iBGP routes? BGP prefers eBGP routes over iBGP routes because routes learned from external peers are generally assumed to be closer to their destination. An eBGP route has already left the local AS and is being propagated by an external neighbor, while an iBGP route represents a path that must still exit the AS via some internal path. This preference is evaluated after LOCAL_PREF, AS_PATH, ORIGIN, and MED, so it can be overridden by policy. Can I change the BGP path selection algorithm? You cannot change the fundamental order of attribute evaluation in the BGP algorithm, but you can influence which path wins by manipulating the attributes themselves through routing policy. Techniques include setting LOCAL_PREF via route maps, using AS_PATH prepending, setting MED for inbound traffic management, and using communities to signal peers. Some vendors also offer vendor-specific attributes (like Cisco's WEIGHT) that are evaluated before the standard attributes. By Denton Chikura Technical Writer Denton Chikura is a technical writer and longtime observability advocate focused on helping site reliability engineers and engineering teams discover the tools and capabilities that strengthen internet resilience. He works at the intersection of monitoring, performance, and infrastructure to make complex systems more understandable and usable, bridging the gap between deep technical detail and real‑world operations. His goal is to help teams build faster, detect issues earlier, and recover smarter, ultimately making the internet a better, more reliable place for everyone. Determined by the vPC role priority (vpc role priority command) Lower value = higher priority Default – 32667 If priorities are equal, the switch with the lower system MAC wins.

Auto-derived Route Distinguisher (rd auto) is based on the Type 1 encoding format as described in IETF RFC 4364 section 4.2. The Type 1 encoding allows – 4-byte administrative field 2-byte numbering field In NXOS with auto-rd – Administrative field is derived from the BGP router-id. Numbering field For IP-VRF – VRF ID For MAC-VRF – 32767 + VLAN ID Administrative field 4 bytes Numbering field 2 bytes Example auto-derived Route Distinguisher (RD) – IP-VRF with BGP Router ID 192.0.2.1 and VRF ID 6 – RD 192.0.2.1:6 MAC-VRF with BGP Router ID 192.0.2.1 and VLAN 20 – RD 192.0.2.1:32787 192.168.0.2 32787 192.168.0.2 6 Route Distinguisher (RD) FYI

vPC with Fabric Peering for VXLAN BGP EVPN
Server Server Server Virtual Peer Link Peer Keepalive vPC Domain Leaf2 Leaf3 Spine Spine
Leaf2 Leaf3 Spine Spine vPC with Fabric Peering for VXLAN BGP EVPN
Server Server Server Virtual Peer Link over L3 Fabric (Spine) Peer Keepalive Virtual Peer Link over Fabric (Layer-3) Uses Spines for Redundancy, Resiliency and Performance Doesn’t use VTEP IP address (loopback) Peer Keepalive remains Out-of-Band (mgmt0 or dedicated link) In-Band (dedicated Loopback over Fabric) vPC Domain Mark CFS messages with specific DSCP value Classify CFS traffic by matching DSCP value, and put into strict priority class

Infrastructure VLANs are used for Backup Routing Path, in default VRF Infrastructure VLAN is present on Peer Link Used in case of failure of uplinks on a vPC peer Required for BUM traffic transfer vPC Infrastructure VLANs VTEP VTEP Baremetal Spine Spine Overlay Nexus (config)# system nve infra-vlans <1-3967>
Infrastructure VLANs are used for Backup Routing Path, in default VRF Infrastructure VLAN is present on Peer Link Used in case of failure of uplinks on a vPC peer Required for BUM traffic transfer vPC Infrastructure VLANs VTEP VTEP Baremetal Spine Spine Overlay Nexus (config)# system nve infra-vlans <1-3967>
vPC Delay Restore After vPC peer reload, traffic might be black-holed, before L3 connectivity is reestablished vPC link bring up can be delayed to allow Underlay and Overlay Convergence Allows encapsulation path to converge Default time 150 seconds Nexus(config-vpc-domain)# delay restore <1-3600 sec> Overlay VTEP VTEP Baremetal
Orphan Port Delay Restore After vPC peer reload, traffic might be black-holed, before L3 connectivity is reestablished Orphan port bring up can be delayed to allow Underlay and Overlay Convergence Allows encapsulation path to converge Default time is equal as vPC delay restore time Nexus(config-vpc-domain)# delay restore orphan-port <0-300 sec> Overlay VTEP VTEP Baremetal Baremetal
Distributed Anycast Gateway SVI Delay Restore After vPC peer reload, traffic might be black-holed, before L3 connectivity is reestablished SVI bring up can be delayed to allow Underlay and Overlay Convergence Allows encapsulation path to converge Default time 10 seconds Nexus (config-vpc-domain)# delay restore interface-vlan <1-3600 sec> Overlay VTEP VTEP Baremetal SVI 1 SVI 1
NVE Hold-Down Timer After vPC peer reload, traffic going to Anycast VTEP hashed to the peer will be black-holed Advertisements of NVE loopback interface can be suppressed until overlay has converged NVE loopback interface bring up can be delayed using hold-down timer For proper overlay convergence, holddown time needs to be longer than delay restore time Default time 180 seconds Nexus(config-if-nve)# source-interface hold-down <1-1500 sec> Overlay VTEP VTEP Baremetal NVE 1 NVE 1
vPC Timers Implication When vPC peer reloads interfaces are down: Peer Keepalive Link Peer Link vPC member ports Orphan Ports Distributed Anycast Gateway SVI NVE On the bring up timers are triggered before interfaces are brought up Spine Leaf Overlay VPC VTEP VTEP Baremetal Baremetal VTEP Baremetal NVE SVI NVE SVI
Optimize Convergence for Orphan ports After vPC peer reload, NVE sourceloopback is kept down by using holddown timer to avoid traffic blackholing During this period, no traffic (vPC and Orphan) is attracted by this peer. While the dual-attached hosts are being served by the other peer It delays convergence of Orphan Type-2 & Type-5 routes which are advertised using PIP. Overlay VTEP VTEP Baremetal NVE 1 NVE 1
Optimize Convergence for Orphan ports Split Loopbacks
VTEP’s PIP and VIP are assigned to separate loopbacks It allows PIP loopback to brought up before the VIP (Anycast) loopback. Improves convergence for following routes advertised using PIP - Type-2 Orphan Type-5 subnet routes (local) Overlay VTEP VTEP Baremetal NVE 1 NVE 1 NXOS 10.4.1
Optimize Convergence for Orphan ports Split Loopbacks
Bringing up PIP loopback interface is controlled by Fabric Ready timer Default is 3/4th of source-interface hold-down-timer (i.e. 135 seconds) Overlay VTEP VTEP Baremetal NVE 1 NVE 1 Nexus(config-if-nve)# fabric-ready time <1-1200 sec> NXOS 10.4.1

VXLAN vPC Consistency Checking vPC consistency check in VXLAN requires: The same VLAN-to-VNI mapping on both vPC peers SVI present for VLANs mapped to VNI on both vPC peers The same VNI needs to use the same BUM traffic transport mechanism on both VTEPs When a VNI uses multicast replication, both VTEPs need to use the same multicast group for this VNI When vPC VTEP consistency check failed: The NVE loopback interface will be admin shutdown on the vPC secondary VTEP

Migrating Legacy DCs to VXLAN EVPN Fabrics Step 1 - Insert a Pair of vPC BGWs in Each Legacy Site
▪ Recommended to deploy double-sided vPC connections between legacy aggregation devices and vPC BGWs ▪ Default gateway functions still offered on the legacy aggregation devices (Active/Standby across sites) Legacy Site 2 VTEP VTEP Legacy Site 1 VTEP VTEP BGW BGW vPCvPC BGW BGW Double-sided vPC connections Dou l  con L3 L2 L3 L2
Migrating Legacy DCs to VXLAN EVPN Fabrics Step 2 - Configure vPC BGWs DCI Underlay Peering
▪ Establish underlay routing adjacencies with the first-hop L3 devices in the core network ➢ EBGP is the recommended protocol of choice ➢ Establish EBGP point-to-point peerings using the physical interfaces IP addresses ▪ Underlay connectivity across the core network required to exchange BGW loopback addresses with the remote vPC BGWs Legacy Site 2 VTEP VTEP Legacy Site 1 VTEP VTEP BGW BGW vPCvPC BGW BGW EBGP EBGP
Migrating Legacy DCs to VXLAN EVPN Fabrics Step 3 - Configure vPC BGWs DCI Overlay Peering
▪ Establish overlay routing adjacencies between vPC BGWs deployed in separate sites ➢ Mandatory establishment of EBGP session across sites ➢ Full-mesh EBGP peering is required ➢ Alternatively, can use route-server services in the core network Legacy Site 2 VTEP VTEP Legacy Site 1 VTEP VTEP BGW BGW vPCvPC BGW BGW MP-EBGP - EVPN
Migrating Legacy DCs to VXLAN EVPN Fabrics Step 4 - Configure vPC BGWs for DCI Layer 2 Extension across Sites
▪ Layer-2 extension can now start being performed between vPC BGWs pairs ➢ Add the VLANs that need to be extended on the L2 trunk between legacy network and vPC BGWs ➢ Map the VLANs to L2VNI segments on the vPC BGW devices ➢ MAC information would start being advertised across sites for endpoints connected to those VLANs Legacy Site 2 VTEP VTEP Legacy Site 1 VTEP VTEP BGW BGW vPCvPC BGW BGW L3 L2 L3 L2 Baremetal Baremetal VLAN 10 VLAN 10 Add VLAN 10 to L2 trunk Add VLAN 10 to L2 trunk Map VLAN 10 to L2VNI 5010 Map VLAN 10 to L2VNI 5010 Layer-2 Extension via VXLAN
Migrating Legacy DCs to VXLAN EVPN Fabrics Step 4 - Configure vPC BGWs for DCI Layer 2 Extension across Sites
▪ Endpoints connected to the legacy network are discovered as directly connected to the local vPC BGW pair ▪ VXLAN tunnels for intersite Layer-2 connectivity are established between the vPC VIP addresses Legacy Site 2 VTEP VTEP Legacy Site 1 VTEP VTEP BGW BGW vPCvPC BGW BGW L3 L2 L3 L2 Layer-2 Extension via VXLAN Baremetal Host 1 0000.3010.1101 192.168.10.101 Baremetal Host 2 0000.3010.1102 192.168.10.102 MAC NH 0000.3010.1101 Po1 0000.3010.1102 vPC VIP2 MAC NH 0000.3010.1101 vPC VIP1 0000.3010.1102 Po2 Po1 Po2 All End-Points in the legacy network are learned as directly connected to the BGWs All endpoints i  the legacy network ar  lea ned as directly connected to the BGWs
Migrating Legacy DCs to VXLAN EVPN Fabrics Step 4 – Potential Creation of End-to-End Layer 2 Loops
▪ If a traditional DCI solution (OTV, VPLS, etc.) is already deployed between the legacy sites, it is possible to end up with the creation of an end-to-end Layer-2 loop ▪ STP BPDUs are not forwarded via Multi-Site, so extending the same VLAN via traditional and NextGen DCI solutions at the same time should be avoided Legacy Site 2 VTEP VTEP Legacy Site 1 VTEP VTEP BGW BGW vPCvPC BGW BGW L3 L2 L3 L2 Layer-2 Extension via VXLAN Baremetal Baremetal Po1 Po2 Legacy DCI extending VLAN 10 L2 Loop! Migrating Legacy DCs to VXLAN EVPN Fabrics Step 5 - Migrate Default Gateway to the vPC BGWs
Default-gateway can be migrated on a per-VLAN basis, anytime Disruptive: (Legacy SVI shut -> DAG SVI no shut) Non-disruptive: Requires DAG/HSRP coexistence (NX-OS 10.2(3) and later!) Classic Ethernet to VXLAN Migration Default Gateway Placement Considerations
Prior to NX-OS 10.2(3) FHRP and Distributed Anycast Gateway (DAG) cannot co-exist FHRP and DAG run in different modes. CE/FP uses HSRP/VRRP while VXLAN uses anycast gateway They cannot be active simultaneously With HSRP the MAC is derived from the HSRP version and the group. In VXLAN EVPN the DAG virtual MAC is configured globally, that means all VLANs have the same MAC for the default gateway Two options: Keep default gateway in the CE/FP network until migration is finished or migrate gateways to the VXLAN EVPN fabric prior to workload migration MAC alignment needs to be done in either mode EP1 EP2 EP3 VXLAN BGP EVPN Fabric Classic Ethernet “Layer-2 Stretch” What is the MAC of Default Gateway IP? FHRP 10.1.1.1 > 0000.0C9F.F001 VXLAN Anycast 10.1.1.1 > 2020.0000.00aa Aggregation Access Leaf Spine Border
Classic Ethernet to VXLAN Migration First Hop Gateway Premigration Steps - MAC alignment
Manually updating ARP cache isn’t feasible Prior to any migration HSRP VMAC needs to be changed to the DAG MAC Hosts need to be updated by changing the standby HSRP member to active to force a GARP* *There’s a possibility that not all hosts ARP cache gets updated **All pre-migration steps should be performed during a maintenance window HSRP VMAC configuration on Aggregation interface vlan 20 vrf member Tenant-A ip address 192.168.20.201/24 hsrp 10 ip 192.168.20.1 mac-address 2020.0000.00aa HSRP VMAC configuration on Aggregation interface vlan 20 vrf member Tenant-A ip address 192.168.20.201/24 hsrp 10 ip 192.168.20.1 mac-address 2020.0000.00aa priority 120 preempt

Today TCP Connection Management Announcements Quiz3 in class on 03/26/2026 Lab3 due on 03/27/2026 Last Transport Introduction Recap Key Questions: What functionalities does the transport layer provide? Terminology UDP TCP Three-way handshake Physical layer Link layer IP layer Transport layer Application layer Host-to-host communications between two endpoints Applications run as processes within a host Transport Layer in the TCP/IP Model What functionalities does the transport layer provide? Process-to-process communication channels Q1: How to set up the process-to-process channel? Q2: How to multiplex concurrent channels over the physical link? Q3: How to control the transmission rate? Q4: How to achieve reliable delivery? Q5: How to share the in-network bandwidth resources? UDP Issues #1: Arbitrary communication Senders and receivers can talk to each other in any ways #2: No reliability guarantee Packets can be lost/duplicated/reordered during transmission A checksum is not enough #3: No resource management Each channel works as an exclusive network resource owner No adaptive support for the physical networks and applications UDP Issues #1: Arbitrary communication Senders and receivers can talk to each other in any ways #2: No reliability guarantee Packets can be lost/duplicated/reordered during transmission A checksum is not enough #3: No resource management Each channel works as an exclusive network resource owner No adaptive support for the physical networks and applications On-demand communication What is the goal of TCP connection management? Dynamically create and destroy a full-duplex communication channel between a sender process and a receiver process for  reliable byte stream exchange Client <—> Server Client and server agree on the start of byte steams for two directions SYN, SequenceNum = X Client Server SYN + ACK, SequenceNum = Y Acknowledgement = X + 1 ACK, Acknowledgement = Y + 1 Three-Way Handshake Closed Client Closed Server SYN_SENT Active open/SYN LISTEN Passive open SYN_RCVD SYN/SYN+ACK ESTABLISHED SYN+ACK/ACK ESTABLISHED ACK Three-Way Handshake State Machine SYN, SequenceNum = X Client Receiver SYN + ACK, SequenceNum = Y Acknowledgement = X + 1 ACK, Acknowledgement = Y + 1 Closed Closed SYN_SENT SYN_RCVD ESTABLISHED LISTEN ESTABLISHED

Revisit the TCP Header Options (variable) Data Checksum SrcPort DstPort HdrLen 0 Flags UrgPtr AdvertisedWindow SequenceNum Acknowledgment 0 4 10 16 31 If SYN flag is set, this is the initial sequence number. The start of a byte stream. If SYN flag is clear, this is the accumulated sequence number of the first data byte of this segment for the current session. Revisit the TCP Header Options (variable) Data Checksum SrcPort DstPort HdrLen 0 Flags UrgPtr AdvertisedWindow SequenceNum Acknowledgment 0 4 10 16 31 If ACK flag is set, the value of this field is the next sequence number that the sender of the ACK is expecting. This acknowledges receipt of all prior bytes (if any). The first ACK sent by each end acknowledges the other end’s initial sequence number itself, but no data. Revisit the TCP Header TCP Connection Management Summary
#1: Connection setup is asymmetric One side does a passive open and the other side does an active open #2: Connection teardown is symmetric Each side has to close the connection independently TCP Connection Management Summary
#1: Connection setup is asymmetric One side does a passive open and the other side does an active open #2: Connection teardown is symmetric Each side has to close the connection independently #3: Most states schedule a timeout Timeouts are triggered when the expected responses does not happen TCP Connection Management Summary
#1: Connection setup is asymmetric One side does a passive open and the other side does an active open #2: Connection teardown is symmetric Each side has to close the connection independently #3: Most states schedule a timeout Timeouts are triggered when the expected responses does not happen TCP(UDP) Connection = Flow The network processing granularity in the transport layer Five tuples = (src IP, dst IP, protocol number, src port, dst port)
How does TCP solve the first issue? #1: Arbitrary communication Senders and receivers can talk to each other in any ways #2: No reliability guarantee Packets can be lost/duplicated/reordered during transmission A checksum is not enough #3: No resource management Each channel works as an exclusive network resource owner No adaptive support for the physical networks and applications TCP avoids arbitrary communication but exposes non-negligible attacking interfaces. SYN Flood Attack
Expected behavior: The TCP connection establishment phase starts with a standardized three-way handshake. The client sends a SYN packet. The server responds with a SYN-ACK. SYN Flood Attack
Expected behavior: The TCP connection establishment phase starts with a standardized three-way handshake. The client sends a SYN packet. The server responds with a SYN-ACK. SYN Flood Attack
Abnormal behavior: An attacker sends an overwhelming number of SYN requests and intentionally never responds to the server’s SYN-ACK messages. Expected behavior: The TCP connection establishment phase starts with a standardized three-way handshake. The client sends a SYN packet. The server responds with a SYN-ACK.
